          <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">

         <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet#Fonts" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">

         </a>
         , you may see
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Replacement_character" title="Replacement character">

         </a>
         instead of
         <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">

         </a>

         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">
          Help:IPA
         </a>
         .
        </td>
       </tr>

      <p>
       <b>
        Turkish
       </b>
       (
       <span class="unicode haudio">
        <span class="fn">
         <span style="white-space:nowrap">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Turkce.ogg" title="About this sound">
           <img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/>
          </a>
         </span>
         <a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/97/Turkce.ogg" title="Turkce.ogg">
          <i>
           Türkçe
          </i>
         </a>
        </span>
        <small class="metadata audiolinkinfo" style="cursor:help;">
         (
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">
          <span style="cursor:help;">
           help
          </span>
         </a>
         ·
         <a href="/wiki/File:Turkce.ogg" title="File:Turkce.ogg">
          <span style="cursor:help;">
           info
          </span>
         </a>
         )
        </small>
       </span>
       ), also referred to as
       <b>
        Istanbul Turkish
       </b>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       is the most widely spoken of the
       <a href="/wiki/Turkic_languages" title="Turkic languages">
        Turkic languages
       </a>
       , with around 10–15 million native speakers in
       <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Europe" title="Southeast Europe">
        Southeast Europe
       </a>
       (mostly in
       <a href="/wiki/East_Thrace" title="East Thrace">
        East
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Western_Thrace" title="Western Thrace">
        Western Thrace
       </a>
       ) and 60–65 million native speakers in
       <a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">
        Western Asia
       </a>
       (mostly in
       <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">
        Anatolia
       </a>
       ). Outside
       <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">
        Turkey
       </a>
       , significant smaller groups of speakers exist in Germany, Bulgaria, Macedonia,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-turkic_5-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-turkic-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Northern_Cyprus" title="Northern Cyprus">
        Northern Cyprus
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cyprus_6-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-cyprus-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Greece,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-greece_7-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-greece-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the Caucasus, and other parts of Europe and Central Asia.
       <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">
        Cyprus
       </a>
       has requested that the
       <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">
        European Union
       </a>
       add Turkish as an official EU language, even though Turkey is not a member state.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cyprus-eu_8-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-cyprus-eu-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       To the west, the influence of
       <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Turkish_language" title="Ottoman Turkish language">
        Ottoman Turkish
       </a>
       —the variety of the Turkish language that was used as the administrative and literary language of the
       <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">
        Ottoman Empire
       </a>
       —spread as the Ottoman Empire expanded. In 1928, as one of
       <a href="/wiki/Atat%C3%BCrk%27s_Reforms" title="Atatürk's Reforms">
        Atatürk's Reforms
       </a>
       in the early years of the Republic of Turkey, the
       <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Turkish_alphabet" title="Ottoman Turkish alphabet">
        Ottoman Turkish alphabet
       </a>
       was replaced with a
       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_alphabet" title="Turkish alphabet">
        Latin alphabet
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The distinctive characteristics of the Turkish language are
       <a href="/wiki/Vowel_harmony" title="Vowel harmony">
        vowel harmony
       </a>
       and extensive
       <a href="/wiki/Agglutination" title="Agglutination">
        agglutination
       </a>
       . The basic word order of Turkish is
       <a href="/wiki/Subject%E2%80%93object%E2%80%93verb" title="Subject–object–verb">
        subject–object–verb
       </a>
       . Turkish has no
       <a href="/wiki/Noun_class" title="Noun class">
        noun classes
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">
        grammatical gender
       </a>
       . The language has a strong
       <a href="/wiki/T%E2%80%93V_distinction" title="T–V distinction">
        T–V distinction
       </a>
       and usage of
       <a href="/wiki/Honorific" title="Honorific">
        honorifics
       </a>
       . Turkish uses
       <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_person" title="Grammatical person">
        second-person
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Pronoun" title="Pronoun">
        pronouns
       </a>
       that distinguish varying levels of politeness,
       <a href="/wiki/Social_distance" title="Social distance">
        social distance
       </a>
       , age, courtesy or familiarity toward the addressee. The plural second-person pronoun and verb forms are used referring to a single person out of respect.
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

         </a>
        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-35">
         <a href="#See_also">
          <span class="tocnumber">
           11
          </span>
          <span class="toctext">
           See also
          </span>
         </a>
        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-36">
         <a href="#References">
          <span class="tocnumber">
           12
          </span>
          <span class="toctext">
           References
          </span>
         </a>
        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-37">
         <a href="#Further_reading">
          <span class="tocnumber">
           13
          </span>
          <span class="toctext">
           Further reading
          </span>
         </a>
        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-38">
         <a href="#External_links">
          <span class="tocnumber">
           14
          </span>
          <span class="toctext">
           External links
          </span>
         </a>
        </li>
       </ul>
      </div>
      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">
        Classification
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Turkic_languages" title="Turkic languages">
        Turkic languages
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kyzyl_orkhon_inscription.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="337" height="404" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Kyzyl_orkhon_inscription.jpg/170px-Kyzyl_orkhon_inscription.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Kyzyl_orkhon_inscription.jpg/255px-Kyzyl_orkhon_inscription.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fd/Kyzyl_orkhon_inscription.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kyzyl_orkhon_inscription.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Old_Turkic_language" title="Old Turkic language">
          Old Turkic
         </a>
         inscription with the
         <a href="/wiki/Old_Turkic_alphabet" title="Old Turkic alphabet">
          Old Turkic alphabet
         </a>
         (c. 8th century).
         <a href="/wiki/Kyzyl" title="Kyzyl">
          Kyzyl
         </a>
         , Russia
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       About 40% of all speakers of Turkic languages are native Turkish speakers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-katzner_9-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-katzner-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The characteristic features of Turkish, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Vowel_harmony" title="Vowel harmony">
        vowel harmony
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Agglutination" title="Agglutination">
        agglutination
       </a>
       , and lack of
       <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">
        grammatical gender
       </a>
       , are universal within the Turkic family. The Turkic family comprises some 30 living languages spoken across Eastern Europe, Central Asia, and Siberia.
      </p>
      <p>
       Turkish is a member of the
       <a href="/wiki/Oghuz_languages" title="Oghuz languages">
        Oghuz group of languages
       </a>
       , a subgroup of the
       <a href="/wiki/Turkic_languages" title="Turkic languages">
        Turkic language
       </a>
       family. There is a high degree of
       <a href="/wiki/Mutual_intelligibility" title="Mutual intelligibility">
        mutual intelligibility
       </a>
       between Turkish and the other Oghuz Turkic languages, including
       <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijani_language" title="Azerbaijani language">
        Azerbaijani
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Turkmen_language" title="Turkmen language">
        Turkmen
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Qashqai_language" title="Qashqai language">
        Qashqai
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Gagauz_language" title="Gagauz language">
        Gagauz
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Balkan_Gagauz_Turkish" title="Balkan Gagauz Turkish">
        Balkan Gagauz Turkish
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ucla_10-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ucla-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Turkic languages were grouped into the now discredited
       <a href="/wiki/Altaic_languages" title="Altaic languages">
        Altaic language group
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_people" title="Turkish people">
        Turkish people
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples#History" title="Turkic peoples">
        Turkic peoples § History
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The earliest known
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Turkic_language" title="Old Turkic language">
        Old Turkic inscriptions
       </a>
       are the three monumental
       <a href="/wiki/Orkhon_inscriptions" title="Orkhon inscriptions">
        Orkhon inscriptions
       </a>
       found in modern
       <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">
        Mongolia
       </a>
       . Erected in honour of the prince
       <a href="/wiki/Kul_Tigin" title="Kul Tigin">
        Kul Tigin
       </a>
       and his brother Emperor
       <a href="/wiki/Bilge_Khagan" title="Bilge Khagan">
        Bilge Khagan
       </a>
       , these date back to the second Turk Kaghanate.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-erdal_11-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-erdal-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       . After the discovery and excavation of these monuments and associated stone slabs by Russian archaeologists in the wider area surrounding the
       <a href="/wiki/Orkhon_Valley" title="Orkhon Valley">
        Orkhon Valley
       </a>
       between 1889 and 1893, it became established that the language on the inscriptions was the
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Turkic_language" title="Old Turkic language">
        Old Turkic language
       </a>
       written using the
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Turkic_alphabet" title="Old Turkic alphabet">
        Old Turkic alphabet
       </a>
       , which has also been referred to as "Turkic runes" or "runiform" due to a superficial similarity to the
       <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">
        Germanic
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Runic_alphabet" title="Runic alphabet">
        runic alphabets
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-runiform_12-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-runiform-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       With the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turkic_expansion" title="Turkic expansion">
        Turkic expansion
       </a>
       during Early Middle Ages (c. 6th–11th centuries), peoples speaking Turkic languages spread across
       <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">
        Central Asia
       </a>
       , covering a vast geographical region stretching from
       <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">
        Siberia
       </a>
       and to
       <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">
        Europe
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">
        Mediterranean
       </a>
       . The
       <a href="/wiki/Seljuq_dynasty" title="Seljuq dynasty">
        Seljuqs
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Oghuz_Turks" title="Oghuz Turks">
        Oghuz Turks
       </a>
       , in particular, brought their language,
       <a href="/wiki/Oghuz_languages" title="Oghuz languages">
        Oghuz
       </a>
       —the direct ancestor of today's Turkish language—into
       <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">
        Anatolia
       </a>
       during the 11th century.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Findley_13-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Findley-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Also during the 11th century, an early linguist of the Turkic languages,
       <a href="/wiki/Mahmud_al-Kashgari" title="Mahmud al-Kashgari">
        Mahmud al-Kashgari
       </a>
       from the
       <a href="/wiki/Kara-Khanid_Khanate" title="Kara-Khanid Khanate">
        Kara-Khanid Khanate
       </a>
       , published the first comprehensive Turkic language dictionary and map of the geographical distribution of Turkic speakers in the
       <i>
        Compendium of the Turkic Dialects
       </i>
       (Ottoman Turkish:
       <i>
        Divânü Lügati't-Türk
       </i>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Soucek_14-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Soucek-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Before their adoption of the Turkish language,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatolian_Turks" title="Anatolian Turks">
        Anatolian Turks
       </a>
       are believed to have originally spoken the
       <a href="/wiki/Anatolian_languages" title="Anatolian languages">
        Anatolian languages
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Disputed statement">
          <span title="This claim has reliable sources with contradicting facts (October 2017)">
           disputed
          </span>
         </a>
         <span class="metadata">
          –
          <a href="/wiki/Talk:Turkish_language#Anatolian_Turks" title="Talk:Turkish language">
           discuss
          </a>
         </span>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       This extinct group of languages, which belongs to the
       <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">
        Indo-European
       </a>
       family, was natively spoken by the
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Anatolians" title="Ancient Anatolians">
        Ancient Anatolians
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Ottoman_Turkish">
        Ottoman Turkish
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Turkish_language" title="Ottoman Turkish language">
        Ottoman Turkish language
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Following the adoption of
       <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">
        Islam
       </a>
       c. 950 by the
       <a href="/wiki/Kara-Khanid_Khanate" title="Kara-Khanid Khanate">
        Kara-Khanid Khanate
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Seljuq_dynasty" title="Seljuq dynasty">
        Seljuq Turks
       </a>
       , who are both regarded as the ethnic and cultural ancestors of the
       <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">
        Ottomans
       </a>
       , the administrative language of these states acquired a large collection of loanwords from
       <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">
        Arabic
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">
        Persian
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_literature" title="Turkish literature">
        Turkish literature
       </a>
       during the Ottoman period, particularly
       <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_poetry" title="Ottoman poetry">
        Divan poetry
       </a>
       , was heavily influenced by Persian, including the adoption of poetic meters and a great quantity of imported words. The literary and official language during the
       <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">
        Ottoman Empire
       </a>
       period (c. 1299–1922) is termed
       <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Turkish_language" title="Ottoman Turkish language">
        Ottoman Turkish
       </a>
       , which was a mixture of Turkish, Persian, and Arabic that differed considerably and was largely unintelligible to the period's everyday Turkish. The everyday Turkish, known as
       <i>
        kaba Türkçe
       </i>
       or "rough Turkish", spoken by the less-educated lower and also rural members of society, contained a higher percentage of native vocabulary and served as basis for the modern Turkish language.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Language_reform_and_modern_Turkish">
        Language reform and modern Turkish
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       After the foundation of the modern state of
       <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">
        Turkey
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="#Writing_system">
        script reform
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_Language_Association" title="Turkish Language Association">
        Turkish Language Association
       </a>
       (TDK) was established in 1932 under the patronage of
       <a href="/wiki/Mustafa_Kemal_Atat%C3%BCrk" title="Mustafa Kemal Atatürk">
        Mustafa Kemal Atatürk
       </a>
       , with the aim of conducting research on Turkish. One of the tasks of the newly established association was to initiate a
       <a href="/wiki/Language_reform" title="Language reform">
        language reform
       </a>
       to replace
       <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">
        loanwords
       </a>
       of Arabic and Persian origin with Turkish equivalents.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       By banning the usage of imported words in the press, the association succeeded in removing several hundred foreign words from the language. While most of the words introduced to the language by the TDK were newly derived from Turkic roots, it also opted for reviving Old Turkish words which had not been used for centuries.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TDK_History_18-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-TDK_History-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Owing to this sudden change in the language, older and younger people in Turkey started to differ in their vocabularies. While the generations born before the 1940s tend to use the older terms of Arabic or Persian origin, the younger generations favor new expressions. It is considered particularly ironic that Atatürk himself, in
       <a href="/wiki/Nutuk" title="Nutuk">
        his lengthy speech
       </a>
       to the new
       <a href="/wiki/Grand_National_Assembly_of_Turkey" title="Grand National Assembly of Turkey">
        Parliament
       </a>
       in 1927, used a style of Ottoman which sounded so alien to later listeners that it had to be "translated" three times into modern Turkish: first in 1963, again in 1986, and most recently in 1995.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The past few decades have seen the continuing work of the TDK to coin new Turkish words to express new concepts and technologies as they enter the language, mostly from English. Many of these new words, particularly information technology terms, have received widespread acceptance. However, the TDK is occasionally criticized for coining words which sound contrived and artificial. Some earlier changes—such as
       <i>
        <span lang="tr">
         bölem
        </span>
       </i>
       to replace
       <i>
        <span lang="tr">
         fırka
        </span>
       </i>
       , "political party"—also failed to meet with popular approval (
       <i>
        <span lang="tr">
         fırka
        </span>
       </i>
       has been replaced by the French loanword
       <i>
        <span lang="tr">
         parti
        </span>
       </i>
       ). Some words restored from Old Turkic have taken on specialized meanings; for example
       <i>
        <span lang="tr">
         betik
        </span>
       </i>
       (originally meaning "book") is now used to mean "
       <a href="/wiki/Scripting_language" title="Scripting language">
        script
       </a>
       " in
       <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">
        computer science
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cokbilgi_20-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-cokbilgi-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Many of the words derived by TDK coexist with their older counterparts.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       This usually happens when a loanword changes its original meaning. For instance,
       <i>
        <span lang="tr">
         dert
        </span>
       </i>
       , derived from the Persian
       <i>
        <span lang="fa-Latn">
         dard
        </span>
       </i>
       (
       <span lang="fa">
        درد
       </span>
       "pain"), means "problem" or "trouble" in Turkish; whereas the native Turkish word
       <i>
        <span lang="tr">
         ağrı
        </span>
       </i>
       is used for physical pain. Sometimes the loanword has a slightly different meaning from the native Turkish word, creating a situation similar to the coexistence of
       <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">
        Germanic
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">
        Romance
       </a>
       words in English.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       Some examples of modern Turkish words and the old loanwords are:
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th align="left">
         Ottoman Turkish
        </th>
        <th align="left">
         Modern Turkish
        </th>
        <th align="left">
         English translation
        </th>
        <th align="left">
         Comments
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          <span lang="ota-Latn">
           müselles
          </span>
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          <span lang="tr">
           üçgen
          </span>
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         triangle
        </td>
        <td>
         Compound of the noun
         <i>
          <span lang="tr">
           üç
          </span>
         </i>
         the suffix
         <i>
          <span lang="tr">
           -gen
          </span>
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          <span lang="ota-Latn">
           tayyare
          </span>
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          <span lang="tr">

          </span>

        </td>

         <i>
          <span lang="tr">

          </span>
         </i>
         ("to fly"). The word was first proposed to mean "airport".
        </td>
       </tr>

         <i>

         (to cut).
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>

          </span>

        </td>

         <i>

         <a href="/wiki/Middle_Turkic_languages" title="Middle Turkic languages">
          Middle Turkic
         </a>
         usage.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
          <a href="#cite_note-21">
           [21]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>

          </span>
         </i>
        </td>

         <i>

          </span>
         </i>
        </td>

        </td>
        <td>
         The noun
         <i>
          <span lang="tr">
           ekim
          </span>
         </i>
         means "the action of planting", referring to the planting of cereal seeds in autumn, which is widespread in Turkey
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       For a more comprehensive list, see
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_replaced_loanwords_in_Turkish" title="List of replaced loanwords in Turkish">
        List of replaced loanwords in Turkish
       </a>
       .
      </div>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Geographic_distribution">
        Geographic distribution
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_diaspora" title="Turkish diaspora">
        Turkish diaspora
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Turkish is natively spoken by the
       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_people" title="Turkish people">
        Turkish people
       </a>
       in Turkey and by the
       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_diaspora" title="Turkish diaspora">
        Turkish diaspora
       </a>
       in some 30 other countries. Turkish language is mutually intelligible with
       <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijani_language" title="Azerbaijani language">
        Azerbaijani
       </a>
       and other Turkic languages. In particular, Turkish-speaking minorities exist in countries that formerly (in whole or part) belonged to the
       <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">
        Ottoman Empire
       </a>
       , such as Iraq
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iraq_22-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-iraq-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       , Bulgaria,
       <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">
        Cyprus
       </a>
       , Greece (primarily in
       <a href="/wiki/Western_Thrace" title="Western Thrace">
        Western Thrace
       </a>
       ), the
       <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Macedonia" title="Republic of Macedonia">
        Republic of Macedonia
       </a>
       , Romania, and Serbia. More than two million Turkish speakers live in Germany; and there are significant Turkish-speaking communities in the United States, France, The Netherlands, Austria, Belgium, Switzerland, and the United Kingdom.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ethnologue_Turkish_23-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ethnologue_Turkish-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Due to the
       <a href="/wiki/Cultural_assimilation" title="Cultural assimilation">
        cultural assimilation
       </a>
       of Turkish immigrants in host countries, not all ethnic Turkish immigrants speak the language with native fluency.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 2005, 93% of the population of Turkey were native speakers of Turkish,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eurobarometer_Languages_25-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Eurobarometer_Languages-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       about 67 million at the time, with
       <a href="/wiki/Kurdish_languages" title="Kurdish languages">
        Kurdish languages
       </a>
       making up most of the remainder.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ethnologue_Kurdish_26-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ethnologue_Kurdish-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, most linguistic minorities in Turkey are bilingual, speaking Turkish with native-like fluency.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Official_status">
        Official status
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Prizren_(3DilliTabela).jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1516" data-file-width="2000" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Prizren_%283DilliTabela%29.jpg/220px-Prizren_%283DilliTabela%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Prizren_%283DilliTabela%29.jpg/330px-Prizren_%283DilliTabela%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Prizren_%283DilliTabela%29.jpg/440px-Prizren_%283DilliTabela%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Prizren_(3DilliTabela).jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Road signs in
         <a href="/wiki/Prizren" title="Prizren">
          Prizren
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Kosovo" title="Kosovo">
          Kosovo
         </a>
         . Official languages are:
         <a href="/wiki/Albanian_language" title="Albanian language">
          Albanian
         </a>
         (top),
         <a href="/wiki/Serbian_language" title="Serbian language">
          Serbian
         </a>
         (middle) and Turkish (bottom).
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Turkish is the official language of
       <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">
        Turkey
       </a>
       and is one of the official languages of
       <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">
        Cyprus
       </a>
       . Under the 2008 constitution, Turkish has official status in 38 of
       <a href="/wiki/Kosovo" title="Kosovo">
        Kosovo
       </a>
       's municipalities, including Mamusha
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kosovo_27-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-kosovo-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sabah-kosovo_28-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-sabah-kosovo-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       . and three municipalities of the
       <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Macedonia" title="Republic of Macedonia">
        Republic of Macedonia
       </a>
       , based on the concentration of Turkish-speaking local population.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In Turkey, the regulatory body for Turkish is the
       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_Language_Association" title="Turkish Language Association">
        Turkish Language Association
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        Türk Dil Kurumu
       </i>
       or TDK), which was founded in 1932 under the name
       <i>
        Türk Dili Tetkik Cemiyeti
       </i>
       ("Society for Research on the Turkish Language"). The Turkish Language Association was influenced by the ideology of
       <a href="/wiki/Linguistic_purism" title="Linguistic purism">
        linguistic purism
       </a>
       : indeed one of its primary tasks was the replacement of loanwords and foreign grammatical constructions with equivalents of Turkish origin.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These changes, together with the adoption of the new
       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_alphabet" title="Turkish alphabet">
        Turkish alphabet
       </a>
       in 1928, shaped the
       <a href="#Language_reform_and_modern_Turkish">
        modern Turkish language
       </a>
       spoken today. TDK became an independent body in 1951, with the lifting of the requirement that it should be presided over by the Minister of Education. This status continued until August 1983, when it was again made into a governmental body in the
       <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Turkey" title="Constitution of Turkey">
        constitution of 1982
       </a>
       , following the military
       <a href="/wiki/1980_Turkish_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1980 Turkish coup d'état">
        coup d'état of 1980
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TDK_History_18-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-TDK_History-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Dialects">
        Dialects
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_dialects" title="Turkish dialects">
        Turkish dialects
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Turkey_Turkish_dialects_map_(Main_subgroups)_en.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3508" data-file-width="4961" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Turkey_Turkish_dialects_map_%28Main_subgroups%29_en.jpg/220px-Turkey_Turkish_dialects_map_%28Main_subgroups%29_en.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Turkey_Turkish_dialects_map_%28Main_subgroups%29_en.jpg/330px-Turkey_Turkish_dialects_map_%28Main_subgroups%29_en.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Turkey_Turkish_dialects_map_%28Main_subgroups%29_en.jpg/440px-Turkey_Turkish_dialects_map_%28Main_subgroups%29_en.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Turkey_Turkish_dialects_map_(Main_subgroups)_en.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Map of the main subgroups of
         <a href="/wiki/Turkish_dialects" title="Turkish dialects">
          Turkish dialects
         </a>
         across Southeast Europe and the
         <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">
          Middle East
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Modern standard Turkish is based on the dialect of
       <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">
        Istanbul
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This "Istanbul Turkish" (
       <i>
        İstanbul Türkçesi
       </i>
       ) constitutes the model of written and spoken Turkish, as recommended by
       <a href="/wiki/Ziya_G%C3%B6kalp" title="Ziya Gökalp">
        Ziya Gökalp
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/%C3%96mer_Seyfettin" title="Ömer Seyfettin">
        Ömer Seyfettin
       </a>
       and others.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Dialectal variation persists, in spite of the
       <a href="/wiki/Dialect_levelling" title="Dialect levelling">
        levelling influence
       </a>
       of the standard used in mass media and the
       <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Turkey" title="Education in Turkey">
        Turkish education system
       </a>
       since the 1930s.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johanson_32-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Johanson-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Academically, researchers from Turkey often refer to Turkish dialects as
       <i>
        ağız
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        şive
       </i>
       , leading to an ambiguity with the linguistic concept of
       <a href="/wiki/Accent_(sociolinguistics)" title="Accent (sociolinguistics)">
        accent
       </a>
       , which is also covered with these words. Projects investigating Turkish dialects are being carried out by several universities, as well as a dedicated work group of the Turkish Language Association. Work is currently in progress for the compilation and publication of their research as a comprehensive dialect
       <a href="/wiki/Atlas" title="Atlas">
        atlas
       </a>
       of the Turkish language.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dialects_Workshop_33-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Dialects_Workshop-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dialects_TDK_34-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Dialects_TDK-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        Rumelice
       </i>
       is spoken by
       <a href="/wiki/Immigration_to_Turkey" title="Immigration to Turkey">
        immigrants
       </a>
       from
       <a href="/wiki/Rumelia" title="Rumelia">
        Rumelia
       </a>
       , and includes the distinct dialects of
       <a href="/wiki/Ludogorie" title="Ludogorie">
        Ludogorie
       </a>
       , Dinler, and Adakale, which are influenced by the theoretized
       <a href="/wiki/Balkan_sprachbund" title="Balkan sprachbund">
        Balkan sprachbund
       </a>
       .
       <i>
        Kıbrıs Türkçesi
       </i>
       is the name for
       <a href="/wiki/Cypriot_Turkish" title="Cypriot Turkish">
        Cypriot Turkish
       </a>
       and is spoken by the
       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_Cypriots" title="Turkish Cypriots">
        Turkish Cypriots
       </a>
       .
       <i>
        Edirne
       </i>
       is the dialect of
       <a href="/wiki/Edirne" title="Edirne">
        Edirne
       </a>
       .
       <i>
        Ege
       </i>
       is spoken in the
       <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea" title="Aegean Sea">
        Aegean
       </a>
       region, with its usage extending to
       <a href="/wiki/Antalya" title="Antalya">
        Antalya
       </a>
       . The nomadic
       <a href="/wiki/Y%C3%B6r%C3%BCks" title="Yörüks">
        Yörüks
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Region,_Turkey" title="Mediterranean Region, Turkey">
        Mediterranean Region
       </a>
       of Turkey also have their own dialect of Turkish.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This group is not to be confused with the Yuruk nomads of Macedonia, Greece, and European Turkey who speak
       <a href="/wiki/Balkan_Gagauz_Turkish" title="Balkan Gagauz Turkish">
        Balkan Gagauz Turkish
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        Güneydoğu
       </i>
       is spoken in the southeast, to the east of
       <a href="/wiki/Mersin" title="Mersin">
        Mersin
       </a>
       .
       <i>
        Doğu
       </i>
       , a dialect in the
       <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Anatolia_Region" title="Eastern Anatolia Region">
        Eastern Anatolia Region
       </a>
       , has a
       <a href="/wiki/Dialect_continuum" title="Dialect continuum">
        dialect continuum
       </a>
       . The
       <a href="/wiki/Meskhetian_Turks" title="Meskhetian Turks">
        Meskhetian Turks
       </a>
       who live in Kazakhstan, Azerbaijan and Russia as well as in several Central Asian countries, also speak an Eastern Anatolian dialect of Turkish, originating in the areas of Kars, Ardahan, and Artvin and sharing similarities with
       <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijani_language" title="Azerbaijani language">
        Azerbaijani
       </a>
       , the language of Azerbaijan.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Central_Anatolia_Region" title="Central Anatolia Region">
        Central Anatolia Region
       </a>
       speaks
       <i>
        Orta Anadolu
       </i>
       .
       <i>
        Karadeniz
       </i>
       , spoken in the Eastern
       <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea_Region" title="Black Sea Region">
        Black Sea Region
       </a>
       and represented primarily by the
       <a href="/wiki/Trabzon" title="Trabzon">
        Trabzon
       </a>
       dialect, exhibits
       <a href="/wiki/Stratum_(linguistics)#Substratum" title="Stratum (linguistics)">
        substratum
       </a>
       influence from
       <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">
        Greek
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">
        phonology
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Syntax" title="Syntax">
        syntax
       </a>
       ;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brendemoen_37-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Brendemoen-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       it is also known as
       <i>
        Laz dialect
       </i>
       (not to be confused with the
       <a href="/wiki/Laz_language" title="Laz language">
        Laz language
       </a>
       ).
       <i>
        Kastamonu
       </i>
       is spoken in
       <a href="/wiki/Kastamonu" title="Kastamonu">
        Kastamonu
       </a>
       and its surrounding areas.
       <a href="/wiki/Karamanli_Turkish" title="Karamanli Turkish">
        Karamanli Turkish
       </a>
       is spoken in Greece, where it is called
       <span lang="grc">
        Kαραμανλήδικα
       </span>
       . It is the literary standard for the
       <a href="/wiki/Karamanlides" title="Karamanlides">
        Karamanlides
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">
        Phonology
       </span>
      </h2>
      <table class="metadata mbox-small noprint selfref" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;width: 22em;">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">
          <img alt="[]" data-file-height="100" data-file-width="75" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Parentesi_Quadre.svg/30px-Parentesi_Quadre.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Parentesi_Quadre.svg/45px-Parentesi_Quadre.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Parentesi_Quadre.svg/60px-Parentesi_Quadre.svg.png 2x" width="30"/>
         </a>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="vertical-align:middle; font-size:95%;">
         <b>
          This article contains
          <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">
           IPA
          </a>
          phonetic symbols.
         </b>
         Without proper
         <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet#Fonts" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">
          rendering support
         </a>
         , you may see
         <a href="/wiki/Specials_(Unicode_block)#Replacement_character" title="Specials (Unicode block)">
          question marks, boxes, or other symbols
         </a>
         instead of
         <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">
          Unicode
         </a>
         characters. For an introductory guide on IPA symbols, see
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">
          Help:IPA
         </a>
         .
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_phonology" title="Turkish phonology">
        Turkish phonology
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See
       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_alphabet" title="Turkish alphabet">
        Turkish alphabet
       </a>
       for a pronunciation guide
      </div>
