        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_fricative#Voiceless_alveolar_sibilant" title="Voiceless alveolar fricative">
           s
          </a>
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          <a href="/wiki/Voiced_alveolar_fricative#Voiced_alveolar_sibilant" title="Voiced alveolar fricative">
           z
          </a>
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_postalveolar_fricative" title="Voiceless postalveolar fricative">
           ʃ
          </a>
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          <a href="/wiki/Voiced_postalveolar_fricative" title="Voiced postalveolar fricative">
           ʒ
          </a>
         </span>
        </td>
        <td colspan="2">
        </td>
        <td colspan="2">
        </td>
        <td colspan="2">
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_glottal_fricative" title="Voiceless glottal fricative">
           h
          </a>
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="center">
        <th>
         <a href="/wiki/Approximant_consonant" title="Approximant consonant">
          Approximant
         </a>
        </th>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td colspan="2">
        </td>
        <td colspan="2">
         (
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_lateral_approximant#Velarized_alveolar_lateral_approximant" title="Alveolar lateral approximant">
           ɫ
          </a>
         </span>
         )
        </td>
        <td colspan="2">
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_lateral_approximant" title="Alveolar lateral approximant">
           l
          </a>
         </span>
        </td>
        <td colspan="2">
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          <a href="/wiki/Palatal_approximant#Palatal" title="Palatal approximant">
           j
          </a>
         </span>
        </td>
        <td colspan="2">
        </td>
        <td colspan="2">
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="center">
        <th>
         <a href="/wiki/Flap_consonant" title="Flap consonant">
          Flap
         </a>
        </th>
        <td colspan="2">
        </td>
        <td colspan="2">
        </td>
        <td colspan="2">
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_flap" title="Alveolar flap">
           ɾ
          </a>
         </span>
        </td>
        <td colspan="2">
        </td>
        <td colspan="2">
        </td>
        <td colspan="2">
        </td>
        <td colspan="2">
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       The phoneme that is usually referred to as
       <i>
        yumuşak g
       </i>
       ("soft g"), written ⟨ğ⟩ in Turkish
       <a href="/wiki/Orthography" title="Orthography">
        orthography
       </a>
       , represents a vowel sequence or a rather weak bilabial approximant between rounded vowels, a weak palatal approximant between unrounded front vowels, and a vowel sequence elsewhere. It never occurs at the beginning of a word or a syllable, but always follows a vowel. When word-final or preceding another consonant, it lengthens the preceding vowel.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPA_19993_39-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-IPA_19993-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In native Turkic words, the sounds
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [c]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɟ]
       </span>
       , and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [l]
       </span>
       are in
       <a href="/wiki/Complementary_distribution" title="Complementary distribution">
        complementary distribution
       </a>
       with
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [k]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɡ]
       </span>
       , and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɫ]
       </span>
       ; the former set occurs adjacent to front vowels and the latter adjacent to back vowels. The distribution of these
       <a href="/wiki/Phoneme" title="Phoneme">
        phonemes
       </a>
       is often unpredictable, however, in foreign borrowings and proper nouns. In such words,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [c]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɟ]
       </span>
       , and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [l]
       </span>
       often occur with back vowels:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       some
       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language#Writing_system" title="Turkish language">
        examples
       </a>
       are given below.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Consonant_devoicing">
        Consonant devoicing
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Final-obstruent_devoicing" title="Final-obstruent devoicing">
        Final-obstruent devoicing
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Turkish orthography reflects
       <a href="/wiki/Final-obstruent_devoicing" title="Final-obstruent devoicing">
        final-obstruent devoicing
       </a>
       , whereby a voiced obstruent, such as
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /b d dʒ ɡ/
       </span>
       , is devoiced to
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [p t tʃ k]
       </span>
       at the end of a word or before a consonant, but retains its voicing before a vowel. In loan words, the voiced equivalent of
       <i>
        /k/
       </i>
       is
       <i>
        /g/
       </i>
       ; in native words, it is
       <i>
        /ğ/
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <table class="sortable wikitable">
       <caption>
        Obstruent devoicing in nouns
       </caption>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Underlying
         <br/>
         consonant
        </th>
        <th>
         Devoiced
         <br/>
         form
        </th>
        <th>
         Underlying
         <br/>
         morpheme
        </th>
        <th>
         Dictionary form
        </th>
        <th>
         Dative case /
         <br/>
         1sg present
        </th>
        <th>
         Meaning
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         b
        </td>
        <td>
         p
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          *kitab
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          kitap
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          kitaba
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         book (loan)
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         c
        </td>
        <td>
         ç
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          *uc
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          uç
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          uca
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         tip
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         d
        </td>
        <td>
         t
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          *bud
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          but
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          buda
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         thigh
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         g
        </td>
        <td>
         k
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          *reng
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          renk
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          renge
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         color (loan)
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         ğ
        </td>
        <td>
         k
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          *ekmeğ
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          ekmek
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          ekmeğe
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         bread
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       This is analogous to languages such as
       <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">
        German
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">
        Russian
       </a>
       , but in the case of Turkish, the spelling is usually made to match the sound. However, in a few cases, such as
       <i>
        ad
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /at/
       </span>
       'name' (dative
       <i>
        ada
       </i>
       ), the underlying form is retained in the spelling (cf.
       <i>
        at
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /at/
       </span>
       'horse', dative
       <i>
        ata
       </i>
       ). Other exceptions are
       <i>
        od
       </i>
       'fire' vs.
       <i>
        ot
       </i>
       'herb',
       <i>
        sac
       </i>
       'sheet metal',
       <i>
        saç
       </i>
       'hair'. Most loanwords, such as
       <i>
        kitap
       </i>
       above, are spelled as pronounced, but a few such as
       <i>
        hac
       </i>
       'hajj',
       <i>
        şad
       </i>
       'happy', and
       <i>
        yad
       </i>
       'strange(r)' also show their underlying forms.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Native nouns of two or more syllables that end in
       <i>
        /k/
       </i>
       in dictionary form are nearly all
       <i>
        //ğ//
       </i>
       in underlying form. However, most verbs and monosyllabic nouns are underlyingly
       <i>
        //k//
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Vowels">
        Vowels
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Turkish_vowel_chart.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="980" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Turkish_vowel_chart.svg/250px-Turkish_vowel_chart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Turkish_vowel_chart.svg/375px-Turkish_vowel_chart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Turkish_vowel_chart.svg/500px-Turkish_vowel_chart.svg.png 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Turkish_vowel_chart.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Vowels of Turkish. From
         <a href="#CITEREFZimmerOrgun1999">
          Zimmer &amp; Orgun (1999
         </a>
         :155)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The vowels of the Turkish language are, in their alphabetical order, ⟨a⟩, ⟨e⟩, ⟨
       <a href="/wiki/Dotted_and_dotless_I" title="Dotted and dotless I">
        ı
       </a>
       ⟩, ⟨i⟩, ⟨o⟩, ⟨ö⟩, ⟨u⟩, ⟨ü⟩.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Turkish vowel system can be considered as being three-dimensional, where vowels are characterised by how and where they are articulated focusing on three key features:
       <a href="/wiki/Vowel#Backness" title="Vowel">
        front and back
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Roundedness" title="Roundedness">
        rounded and unrounded
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Vowel#Height" title="Vowel">
        vowel height
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_44-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:12-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Vowels are classified [±back], [±round] and [±high].
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The only
       <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">
        diphthongs
       </a>
       in the language are found in
       <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">
        loanwords
       </a>
       and may be categorised as falling diphthongs usually analyzed as a sequence of /j/ and a vowel.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPA_19993_39-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-IPA_19993-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Vowel_harmony">
        Vowel harmony
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       For more details on this topic, see
       <a href="/wiki/Vowel_harmony" title="Vowel harmony">
        Vowel harmony
       </a>
       .
      </div>
      <table class="wikitable" style="text-align: center;; margin-left: 1em">
       <tr>
        <th rowspan="2">
         Turkish Vowel Harmony
        </th>
        <th colspan="4">
         Front Vowels
        </th>
        <th colspan="4">
         Back Vowels
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th colspan="2">
         Unrounded
        </th>
        <th colspan="2">
         Rounded
        </th>
        <th colspan="2">
         Unrounded
        </th>
        <th colspan="2">
         Rounded
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Vowel
        </th>
        <td style="border-right: 0;">
         <b>
          e
         </b>
         /e/
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          i
         </b>
         /i/
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          ü
         </b>
         /y/
        </td>
        <td style="border-left: 0;">
         <b>
          ö
         </b>
         /ø/
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          a
         </b>
         /a/
        </td>
        <td style="border-left: 0;">
         <b>
          ı
         </b>
         /ɯ/
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          u
         </b>
         /u/
        </td>
        <td style="border-left: 0;">
         <b>
          o
         </b>
         /o/
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr style="text-align: center;">
        <th>
         Twofold (Simple system)
        </th>
        <td colspan="4">
         <b>
          e
         </b>
        </td>
        <td colspan="4">
         <b>
          a
         </b>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr style="text-align: center;">
        <th>
         Fourfold (Complex system)
        </th>
        <td colspan="2">
         <b>
          i
         </b>
        </td>
        <td colspan="2">
         <b>
          ü
         </b>
        </td>
        <td colspan="2">
         <b>
          ı
         </b>
        </td>
        <td colspan="2">
         <b>
          u
         </b>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TurkishRoadSign-WelcomeToEurope_Modified.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="570" data-file-width="500" height="251" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/TurkishRoadSign-WelcomeToEurope_Modified.jpg/220px-TurkishRoadSign-WelcomeToEurope_Modified.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/TurkishRoadSign-WelcomeToEurope_Modified.jpg/330px-TurkishRoadSign-WelcomeToEurope_Modified.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/TurkishRoadSign-WelcomeToEurope_Modified.jpg/440px-TurkishRoadSign-WelcomeToEurope_Modified.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TurkishRoadSign-WelcomeToEurope_Modified.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Road sign at the European end of the
         <a href="/wiki/Bosphorus_Bridge" title="Bosphorus Bridge">
          Bosphorus Bridge
         </a>
         in
         <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">
          Istanbul
         </a>
         . (Photo taken during the 28th
         <a href="/wiki/Istanbul_Marathon" title="Istanbul Marathon">
          Istanbul Marathon
         </a>
         in 2006)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Turkish is an
       <a href="/wiki/Agglutinative_language" title="Agglutinative language">
        agglutinative language
       </a>
       where a series of suffixes are added to the stem word; vowel harmony is a phonological process which ensures a smooth flow, requiring the least amount of oral movement as possible. Vowel harmony can be viewed as a process of
       <a href="/wiki/Assimilation_(phonology)" title="Assimilation (phonology)">
        assimilation
       </a>
       , whereby following vowels take on the characteristics of the preceding vowel.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_46-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:02-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It may be useful to think of Turkish vowels as two symmetrical sets: the a-undotted (a, ı, o, u) which are all back vowels, articulated at the back of the mouth; and the e-dotted (e, i, ö, ü) vowels which are articulated at the front of the mouth. The place and manner of articulation of the vowels will determine which pattern of vowel harmony a word will adopt. The pattern of vowels is shown in the table above.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Grammatical
       <a href="/wiki/Affix" title="Affix">
        affixes
       </a>
       have "a chameleon-like quality",
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and obey one of the following patterns of vowel harmony:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <b>
         twofold (
         <i>
          -e/-a
         </i>
         )
        </b>
        :
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
         <a href="#cite_note-49">
          [49]
         </a>
        </sup>
        the
        <a href="/wiki/Locative_case" title="Locative case">
         locative case
        </a>
        suffix, for example, is
        <i>
         -de
        </i>
        after front vowels and
        <i>
         -da
        </i>
        after back vowels. The notation
        <i>
         -de
        </i>
        ² is a convenient shorthand for this pattern.
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         fourfold (
         <i>
          -i/-ı/-ü/-u
         </i>
         )
        </b>
        : the
        <a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">
         genitive case
        </a>
        suffix, for example, is
        <i>
         -in
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         -ın
        </i>
        after unrounded vowels (front or back respectively); and
        <i>
         -ün
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         -un
        </i>
        after the corresponding rounded vowels. In this case, the shorthand notation
        <i>
         -in
        </i>
        <sup>
         4
        </sup>
        is used.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Practically, the twofold pattern (also referred to as the e-type vowel harmony) means that in the environment where the vowel in the word stem is formed in the front of the mouth, the suffix will take the e-form, while if it is formed in the back it will take the a-form. The fourfold pattern (also called the i-type) accounts for rounding as well as for front/back.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_46-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-:02-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The following examples, based on the
       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_copula" title="Turkish copula">
        copula
       </a>
       <i>
        -dir
       </i>
       <sup>
        4
       </sup>
       ("[it] is"), illustrate the principles of i-type vowel harmony in practice:
       <i>
        Türkiye'
        <b>
         dir
        </b>
       </i>
       ("it is Turkey"),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        kapı
        <b>
         dır
        </b>
       </i>
       ("it is the door"), but
       <i>
        gün
        <b>
         dür
        </b>
       </i>
       ("it is the day"),
       <i>
        palto
        <b>
         dur
        </b>
       </i>
       ("it is the coat").
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
        <a href="#cite_note-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       There are several exceptions to the vowel harmony rules, which can be categorised as follows:
      </p>
      <ol>
       <li>
        A few native root words such as anne (mother), elma (apple) and kardeş (brother). In these cases the suffixes harmonise with the final vowel.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compound_word" title="Compound word">
         Compounds
        </a>
        such as the bu-gün (today) and baş-kent (capital). In these cases vowels are not required to harmonise between the constituent words.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">
         Loanwords
        </a>
        often don't harmonise, however, in some cases the suffixes will harmonise with the front vowel even in words that may not have a front vowel in the final syllable. Usually this occurs when the words end in a palatal [l], for example
        <i>
         halsiz
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         hal
        </i>
        +
        <i>
         -siz
        </i>
        "listless",
        <i>
         meçhuldür
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         meçhul
        </i>
        +
        <i>
         -dir
        </i>
        "it is unknown". However, affixes borrowed from foreign languages do not harmonise, such as -izm (ateizm "atheism"), -en (derived from French -ment as in taxmen "completely), anti- (antidemokratik "antidemocratic").
       </li>
       <li>
        A few native suffixes are also invariable (or at least partially so) such as the second vowel in the bound auxiliary -abil, or in the marker -ken as well as in the imperfect suffix -yor. There are also a few derivational suffixes that do not harmonise such as -gen in uçgen (triangle) or altigen (hexagon).
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_44-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-:12-44">
          [44]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ol>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language#Phonology" title="Turkish language">
        road sign in the photograph
       </a>
       above illustrates several of these features:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        a native compound which does not obey vowel harmony:
        <i>
         Orta+köy
        </i>
        ("middle village"—a place name)
       </li>
       <li>
        a loanword also violating vowel harmony:
        <i>
         viyadük
        </i>
        (&lt; French
        <i>
         viaduc
        </i>
        "viaduct")
       </li>
       <li>
        the possessive suffix
        <i>
         -i
        </i>
        <sup>
         4
        </sup>
        harmonizing with the final vowel (and softening the
        <i>
         k
        </i>
        by consonant
        <a href="/wiki/Alternation_(linguistics)" title="Alternation (linguistics)">
         alternation
        </a>
        ):
        <i>
         viyadüğü
        </i>
        <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
         [
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
           <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2014)">
            citation needed
           </span>
          </a>
         </i>
         ]
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Stress">
        Stress
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       For more details on this topic, see
       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_phonology#Stress" title="Turkish phonology">
        Turkish phonology § Stress
       </a>
       .
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Stress_(linguistics)" title="Stress (linguistics)">
        Stress
       </a>
       is usually on the last syllable in most root words.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPA_19993_39-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-IPA_19993-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There are however, several exceptions. Exceptions include some
       <a href="/wiki/Suffix" title="Suffix">
        suffix
       </a>
       combinations and
       <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">
        loanwords
       </a>
       , particularly from Italian and Greek, as well as interjections, certain question words, adverbs (although not adjectives functioning as adverbs), and many proper names. While the aforementioned loanwords are usually stressed on the penultimate syllable (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɫoˈkanta]
       </span>
       <i>
        lokanta
       </i>
       "restaurant" or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [isˈcele]
       </span>
       <i>
        iskele
       </i>
       "quay"), the stress of proper names is less predictable (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [isˈtanbuɫ]
       </span>
       <i>
        İstanbul
       </i>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ˈaŋkaɾa]
       </span>
       <i>
        Ankara
       </i>
       ) see
       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_phonology#Sezer_stress" title="Turkish phonology">
        Sezer stress
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Syntax">
        Syntax
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Sentence_groups">
        Sentence groups
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Turkish has two groups of sentences: verbal and nominal sentences. In the case of a verbal sentence, the predicate is a finite verb, while the predicate in nominal sentence will have either no overt verb or a verb in the form of the
       <a href="/wiki/Copula_(linguistics)" title="Copula (linguistics)">
        copula
       </a>
       <i>
        ol
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        y
       </i>
       (variants of "be"). Examples of both are given below:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:14_52-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:14-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th>
         Sentence type
        </th>
        <th colspan="2">
         Turkish
        </th>
        <th>
         English
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
         Subject
        </td>
        <td>
         Predicate
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Verbal
        </td>
        <td>
         Necla
        </td>
        <td>
         okula gitti
        </td>
        <td>
         Necla went to school
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Nominal (no verb)
        </td>
        <td>
         Necla
        </td>
        <td>
         oğretmen
        </td>
        <td>
         Necla is a teacher
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         (copula)
        </td>
        <td>
         Necla
        </td>
        <td>
         ev-de-
         <b>
          y
         </b>
         -miş (hyphens delineate suffixes)
        </td>
        <td>
         Apparently Necla is at home
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Negation">
        Negation
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The two groups of sentences have different ways of forming negation. A nominal sentence can be negated with the addition of the word
       <i>
        değil,
       </i>
       for example the sentence above would become
       <i>
        Necla oğretmen değil
       </i>
       (Necla is not a teacher). However, the verbal sentence requires the addition of a negative suffix -me to the verb (the suffix comes after the stem but before the tense):
       <i>
        Necla okula gitmedi
       </i>
       (Necla did not go to school).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:03_53-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:03-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span id="Yes.2Fno_questions">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Yes/no_questions">
        Yes/no questions
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In the case of a verbal sentence, an interrogative morpheme -mi is added to the end of the sentence and stands alone, for example
       <i>
        Necla okula gitti mi?
       </i>
       (Did Necla go to school?). In the case of a nominal sentence, then the -mi comes after the predicate but before the personal ending, so for example
       <i>
        Necla, siz oğretmen misiniz
       </i>
       ? (Necla, are you a teacher? using the formal 2nd person plural).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:03_53-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-:03-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Word_order">
        Word order
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Word order in simple Turkish sentences is generally
       <a href="/wiki/Subject%E2%80%93object%E2%80%93verb" title="Subject–object–verb">
        subject–object–verb
       </a>
       , as in Korean and
       <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
        Latin
       </a>
       , but unlike English, for verbal sentences and subject-predicate for nominal sentences. However, as Turkish possesses a case-marking system, and most grammatical relations are shown using morphological markers, often the SOV structure has diminished relevance, in fact it may be considered a "pragmatic word order" type of language, one that does not rely on word order for grammatical purposes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
        <a href="#cite_note-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       While the basic word order in Turkish is firmly SOV, the word order may vary in particular conditions.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Immediately_preverbal">
        Immediately preverbal
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Consider the following simple sentence which demonstrates that the focus in Turkish is on the element that immediately precedes the verb:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
        <a href="#cite_note-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th>
         Word order
        </th>
        <th>
        </th>
        <th>
        </th>
        <th>
        </th>
        <th>
         Focus
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         SOV
        </td>
        <td>
         Ahmet
         <p>
          Ahmet
         </p>
        </td>
        <td>
         yumurta-yı
         <p>
          egg (accusative)
         </p>
        </td>
        <td>
         yedi
         <p>
          ate
         </p>
        </td>
        <td>
         unmarked: Ahmet ate the egg
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         SVO
        </td>
        <td>
         Ahmet
        </td>
        <td>
         yedi
        </td>
        <td>
         yumurta-yı
        </td>
        <td>
         the focus is on the subject: Ahmet (it was Ahmet who ate the egg)
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         OVS
        </td>
        <td>
         Yumurta-yı
        </td>
        <td>
         yedi
        </td>
        <td>
         Ahmet
        </td>
        <td>
         the focus is on the object: egg (it was an egg that Ahmet ate)
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Postpredicate">
        Postpredicate
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The postpredicate position signifies what is referred to as background information in Turkish- information that is assumed to be known to both the speaker and the listener, or information that is included in the context. Consider the following examples:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:14_52-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-:14-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th>
         Sentence type
        </th>
        <th>
         Word order
        </th>
        <th>
        </th>
        <th>
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Nominal
        </td>
        <td>
         S-predicate
        </td>
        <td>
         Bu ev güzelmiş (apparently this house is beautiful)
        </td>
        <td>
         unmarked
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
         Predicate-s
        </td>
        <td>
         Güzelmiş bu ev (it is apparently beautiful, this house)
        </td>
        <td>
         it is understood that the sentence is about this house
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Verbal
        </td>
        <td>
         SOV
        </td>
        <td>
         Bana da bir kahve getir (get me a coffee too)
        </td>
        <td>
         unmarked
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
         Bana da getir bir kahve (get me one too, a coffee)
        </td>
        <td>
         it is understood that it is a coffee that the speaker wants
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Topic">
        Topic
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       There has been some debate among linguists whether Turkish is a subject-prominent (like English) or
       <a href="/wiki/Topic-prominent_language" title="Topic-prominent language">
        topic-prominent
       </a>
       (like Japanese and Korean) language, with recent scholarship implying that it is indeed both subject and topic-prominent.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This has direct implications for word order as it is possible for the subject to be included in the
       <a href="/wiki/Verb_phrase" title="Verb phrase">
        verb-phrase
       </a>
       in Turkish. There can be S/O inversion in sentences where the topic is of greater importance than the subject.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">
        Grammar
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_grammar" title="Turkish grammar">
        Turkish grammar
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Turkish is an
       <a href="/wiki/Agglutinative_language" title="Agglutinative language">
        agglutinative language
       </a>
       and frequently uses
       <a href="/wiki/Affix" title="Affix">
        affixes
       </a>
       , and specifically suffixes, or endings.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       One word can have many affixes and these can also be used to create new words, such as creating a verb from a noun, or a noun from a verbal root (see the section on
       <a href="#Word_formation">
        Word formation
       </a>
       ). Most affixes indicate the grammatical function of the word.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
        <a href="#cite_note-58">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The only native prefixes are
       <a href="/wiki/Alliteration" title="Alliteration">
        alliterative
       </a>
       intensifying syllables used with adjectives or adverbs: for example
       <i>
        <b>
         sım
        </b>
        sıcak
       </i>
       ("boiling hot" &lt;
       <i>
        sıcak
       </i>
       ) and
       <i>
        <b>
         mas
        </b>
        mavi
       </i>
       ("bright blue" &lt;
       <i>
        mavi
       </i>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
        <a href="#cite_note-59">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The extensive use of affixes can give rise to long words, e.g.
       <i>
        Çekoslovakyalılaştıramadıklarımızdanmışsınızcasına
       </i>
       , meaning "In the manner of you being one of those that we apparently couldn't manage to convert to Czechoslovakian". While this case is contrived, long words frequently occur in normal Turkish, as in this heading of a newspaper obituary column:
       <i>
        Bayramlaşamadıklarımız
       </i>
       (Bayram [festival]-Recipr-Impot-Partic-Plur-PossPl1; "Those of our number with whom we cannot exchange the season's greetings").
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
        <a href="#cite_note-60">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Another example can be seen in the final word of this heading of the online Turkish Spelling Guide (
       <i>
        İmlâ Kılavuzu
       </i>
       ):
       <i>
        Dilde birlik, ulusal birliğin vazgeçilemezlerindendir
       </i>
       ("Unity in language is among the indispensables [dispense-Pass-Impot-Plur-PossS3-Abl-Copula] of national unity ~ Linguistic unity is a
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Sine_qua_non" title="Sine qua non">
         sine qua non
        </a>
       </i>
       of national unity").
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
        <a href="#cite_note-61">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Nouns">
        Nouns
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       There is no
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Definite_article" title="Definite article">
        definite article
       </a>
       in Turkish, but definiteness of the object is implied when the accusative ending is used (see below). Turkish nouns decline by taking case endings. There are six
       <a href="/wiki/Declension" title="Declension">
        noun cases
       </a>
       in Turkish, with all the endings following vowel harmony (shown in the table using the shorthand
       <a href="#Vowel_harmony">
        superscript notation
       </a>
       . The
       <a href="/wiki/Plural" title="Plural">
        plural
       </a>
       marker
       <i>
        -ler
       </i>
       ² immediately follows the noun before any case or other affixes (e.g.
       <i>
        köylerin
       </i>
       "of the villages").
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th rowspan="2">
         Case
        </th>
        <th rowspan="2">
         Ending
        </th>
        <th colspan="2">
         Examples
        </th>
        <th rowspan="2">
         Meaning
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         <i>
          köy
         </i>
         "village"
        </th>
        <th>
         <i>
          ağaç
         </i>
         "tree"
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Nominative_case" title="Nominative case">
          Nominative
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         Ø (none)
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          köy
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          ağaç
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         (the) village/tree
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">
          Genitive
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          -in
         </i>
         <sup>
          4
         </sup>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          köyün
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          ağa
          <b>
           c
          </b>
          ın
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         the village's/tree's
         <br/>
         of the village/tree
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Dative_case" title="Dative case">
          Dative
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          -e
         </i>
         ²
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          köye
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          ağa
          <b>
           c
          </b>
          a
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         to the village/tree
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Accusative_case" title="Accusative case">
          Accusative
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          -i
         </i>
         <sup>
          4
         </sup>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          köyü
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          ağa
          <b>
           c
          </b>
          ı
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         the village/tree
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Ablative_case" title="Ablative case">
          Ablative
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          -den
         </i>
         ²
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          köyden
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          ağaç
          <b>
           t
          </b>
          an
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         from the village/tree
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Locative_case" title="Locative case">
          Locative
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          -de
         </i>
         ²
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          köyde
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          ağaç
          <b>
           t
          </b>
          a
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         in the village/on the tree
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       The accusative case marker is used only for definite objects; compare
       <i>
        (bir) ağaç gördük
       </i>
       "we saw
       <b>
        a
       </b>
       tree" with
       <i>
        ağacı gördük
       </i>
       "we saw
       <b>
        the
       </b>
       tree".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
        <a href="#cite_note-62">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The plural marker
       <i>
        -ler
       </i>
       ² is generally not used when a class or category is meant:
       <i>
        ağaç gördük
       </i>
       can equally well mean "we saw trees [as we walked through the forest]"—as opposed to
       <i>
        ağaçları gördük
       </i>
       "we saw the trees [in question]".
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2013)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The declension of
       <i>
        ağaç
       </i>
       illustrates two important features of Turkish phonology: consonant
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assimilation_(linguistics)" title="Assimilation (linguistics)">
        assimilation
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Suffix" title="Suffix">
        suffixes
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        ağaç
        <b>
         t
        </b>
        an, ağaç
        <b>
         t
        </b>
        a
       </i>
       ) and
       <a href="/wiki/Voice_(phonetics)" title="Voice (phonetics)">
        voicing
       </a>
       of final consonants before vowels (
       <i>
        ağa
        <b>
         c
        </b>
        ın, ağa
        <b>
         c
        </b>
        a, ağa
        <b>
         c
        </b>
        ı
       </i>
       ).
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2013)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Additionally, nouns can take suffixes that assign
       <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_person" title="Grammatical person">
        person
       </a>
       : for example
       <i>
        -imiz
       </i>
       <sup>
        4
       </sup>
       , "our". With the addition of the
       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_copula" title="Turkish copula">
        copula
       </a>
       (for example
       <i>
        -im
       </i>
       <sup>
        4
       </sup>
       , "I am") complete sentences can be formed. The
       <a href="/wiki/Interrogative_word" title="Interrogative word">
        interrogative
       </a>
       particle
       <i>
        mi
       </i>
       <sup>
        4
       </sup>
       immediately follows the word being questioned:
       <i>
        köye mi?
       </i>
       "[going] to the village?",
       <i>
        ağaç mı?
       </i>
       "[is it a] tree?".
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2013)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th>
         Turkish
        </th>
        <th>
         English
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          ev
         </i>
        </td>
        <td align="right">
         (the) house
        </td>
       </tr>

        </td>
       </tr>

        </td>
       </tr>

         my house
        </td>
       </tr>

        </td>

        </td>

         </i>

         I was (apparently) at your house.
        </td>
       </tr>

         Am I at your house?
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_pronouns">
        Personal pronouns
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The Turkish
       <a href="/wiki/Personal_pronoun" title="Personal pronoun">
        personal pronouns
       </a>
       in the nominative case are
       <i>
        ben
       </i>
       (1s),
       <i>
        sen
       </i>
       (2s),
       <i>
        o
       </i>
       (3s),
       <i>
        biz
       </i>
       (1pl),
       <i>
        siz
       </i>
       (2pl, or formal/polite 2s), and
       <i>
        onlar
       </i>
       (3pl). They are declined regularly with some exceptions:
       <i>
        benim
       </i>
       (1s gen.);
       <i>
        bizim
       </i>
       (1pl gen.);
       <i>
        bana
       </i>
       (1s dat.);
       <i>
        sana
       </i>
       (2s dat.); and the oblique forms of
       <i>
        o
       </i>
       use the root
       <i>
        on
       </i>
       . All other pronouns (reflexive
       <i>
        kendi
       </i>
       and so on) are declined regularly.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span id="Noun_phrases_.28tamlama.29">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Noun_phrases_(tamlama)">
        Noun phrases (
        <i>
         tamlama
        </i>
        )
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Two nouns, or groups of nouns, may be joined in either of two ways:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        definite (possessive) compound (
        <i>
         belirtili tamlama
        </i>
        ). E.g.
        <i>
         Türkiye'nin sesi
        </i>
        "the voice of Turkey (radio station)": the voice belonging to Turkey. Here the relationship is shown by the genitive ending
        <i>
         -in
        </i>
        <sup>
         4
        </sup>
        added to the first noun; the second noun has the third-person suffix of possession
        <i>
         -(s)i
        </i>
        <sup>
         4
        </sup>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        indefinite (qualifying) compound (
        <i>
         belirtisiz tamlama
        </i>
        ). E.g.
        <i>
         Türkiye Cumhuriyeti
        </i>
        "Turkey-Republic
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
         <a href="#cite_note-63">
          [63]
         </a>
        </sup>
        = the Republic of Turkey": not the republic belonging to Turkey, but the Republic that is Turkey. Here the first noun has no ending; but the second noun has the ending
        <i>
         -(s)i
        </i>
        <sup>
         4
        </sup>
        —the same as in definite compounds.
        <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
         [
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
           <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2014)">
            citation needed
           </span>
          </a>
         </i>
         ]
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       The following table illustrates these principles.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
        <a href="#cite_note-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In some cases the constituents of the compounds are themselves compounds; for clarity these subsidiary compounds are marked with [square brackets]. The suffixes involved in the linking are underlined. Note that if the second noun group already had a possessive suffix (because it is a compound by itself), no further suffix is added.
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable" style="margin-left:1em;">

        Linked nouns and noun groups

        </th>

         <a href="/wiki/Parvenu" title="Parvenu">

         </a>
         who gives himself airs
        </td>

      <p>
       As the last example shows, the qualifying expression may be a substantival sentence rather than a noun or noun group.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
        <a href="#cite_note-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       There is a third way of linking the nouns where both nouns take no suffixes (
       <i>
        takısız tamlama
       </i>
       ). However, in this case the first noun acts as an adjective,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
        <a href="#cite_note-69">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       e.g.
       <i>
        Demir kapı
       </i>
       (iron gate),
       <i>
        elma yanak
       </i>
       ("apple cheek", i.e. red cheek),
       <i>
        kömür göz
       </i>
       ("coal eye", i.e. black eye) :
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Adjectives">
        Adjectives
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Turkish adjectives are not
       <a href="/wiki/Declension" title="Declension">
        declined
       </a>
       . However most adjectives can also be used as nouns, in which case they are declined: e.g.
       <i>
        güzel
       </i>
       ("beautiful") →
       <i>
        güzeller
       </i>
       ("(the) beautiful ones / people"). Used attributively, adjectives precede the nouns they modify. The adjectives
       <i>
        var
       </i>
       ("existent") and
       <i>
        yok
       </i>
       ("
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-existent" title="Non-existent">
        non-existent
       </a>
       ") are used in many cases where English would use "there is" or "have",
       <i>
        e.g.
       </i>
       <i>
        süt yok
       </i>
       ("there is no milk",
       <i>
        lit.
       </i>
       "(the) milk (is) non-existent"); the construction "
       <i>
        noun 1
       </i>
       -GEN
       <i>
        noun 2
       </i>
       -POSS var/yok" can be translated "
       <i>
        noun 1
       </i>
       has/doesn't have
       <i>
        noun 2
       </i>
       ";
       <i>
        imparatorun elbisesi yok
       </i>
       "the emperor has no clothes" ("(the) emperor-
       <i>
        of
       </i>
       clothes-
       <i>
        his
       </i>
       non-existent");
       <i>
        kedimin ayakkabıları yoktu
       </i>
       ("my cat had no shoes",
       <i>
        lit.
       </i>
       "cat-
       <i>
        my
       </i>
       -
       <i>
        of
       </i>
       shoe-
       <i>
        plur.
       </i>
       -
       <i>
        its
       </i>
       non-existent-
       <i>
        past tense
       </i>
       ").
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Verbs">
        Verbs
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_copula" title="Turkish copula">
        Turkish copula
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Turkish verbs indicate
       <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_person" title="Grammatical person">
        person
       </a>
       . They can be made negative, potential ("can"), or impotential ("cannot"). Furthermore, Turkish verbs show
       <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_tense" title="Grammatical tense">
        tense
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Present_tense" title="Present tense">
        present
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Past_tense" title="Past tense">
        past
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Future_tense" title="Future tense">
        future
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Aorist" title="Aorist">
        aorist
       </a>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_mood" title="Grammatical mood">
        mood
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Conditional_mood" title="Conditional mood">
        conditional
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Imperative_mood" title="Imperative mood">
        imperative
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Inferential_mood" title="Inferential mood">
        inferential
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Necessitative_mood" title="Necessitative mood">
        necessitative
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Optative_mood" title="Optative mood">
        optative
       </a>
       ), and
       <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_aspect" title="Grammatical aspect">
        aspect
       </a>
       . Negation is expressed by the
       <a href="/wiki/Infix" title="Infix">
        infix
       </a>
       <i>
        -me²-
       </i>
       immediately following the stem.
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th>
         Turkish
        </th>
        <th>
         English
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          gel-
         </i>
        </td>
        <td align="right">
         (to) come
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          gelebil-
         </i>
        </td>
        <td align="right">
         (to) be able to come
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          gelme-
         </i>
        </td>
        <td align="right">
         not (to) come
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          geleme-
         </i>
        </td>
        <td align="right">
         (to) be unable to come
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          gelememiş
         </i>
        </td>
        <td align="right">
         Apparently (s)he couldn't come
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          gelebilecek
         </i>
        </td>
        <td align="right">
         (s)he'll be able to come
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          gelmeyebilir
         </i>
        </td>
        <td align="right">
         (s)he may (possibly) not come
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          gelebilirsen
         </i>
        </td>
        <td align="right">
         if thou can come
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          gelinir
         </i>
        </td>
        <td align="right">
         (
         <i>
          passive
         </i>
         ) one comes, people come
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          gelebilmeliydin
         </i>
        </td>
        <td align="right">
         thou shouldst have been able to come
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          gelebilseydin
         </i>
        </td>
        <td align="right">
         if thou could have come
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          gelmeliydin
         </i>
        </td>
        <td align="right">
         thou shouldst have come
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Almost all Turkish verbs are conjugated in the same way, most notable exception being the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Irregular_verbs" title="Irregular verbs">
        irregular
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Defective_verb" title="Defective verb">
        defective verb
       </a>
       <i>
        i-
       </i>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_copula" title="Turkish copula">
        Turkish copula
       </a>
       (corresponding to English
       <i>
        to be
       </i>
       ), which can be used in compound forms (the shortened form is called an
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enclitic" title="Enclitic">
        enclitic
       </a>
       ):
       <i>
        Gelememişti
       </i>
       =
       <i>
        Gelememiş idi
       </i>
       =
       <i>
        Gelememiş + i- + -di
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Verb_tenses">
        Verb tenses
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       There are 9 simple and 20 compound tenses in Turkish. 9 simple tenses are simple past (
       <i>
        di'li geçmiş
       </i>
       ), inferential past (
       <i>
        miş'li geçmiş
       </i>
       ), present continuous, simple present (aorist), future, wish, demand, necessitative ("must") and order.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
        <a href="#cite_note-70">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There are three groups of compound forms. Story (
       <i>
        hikaye
       </i>
       ) is the witnessed past of the above forms (except command), rumor (
       <i>
        rivayet
       </i>
       ) is the unwitnessed past of the above forms (except inferential past and command), conditional wish (
       <i>
        koşul
       </i>
       ) is the conditional form of the first five basic tenses.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
        <a href="#cite_note-71">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In the example below the second person singular of the verb
       <i>
        git
       </i>
       ("go") is shown.
      </p>
      <table class="sortable wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th>
         English of the basic form
        </th>
        <th>
         Basic tense
        </th>
        <th>
         Story (
         <i>
          hikaye
         </i>
         )
        </th>
        <th>
         Rumor (
         <i>
          rivayet
         </i>
         )
        </th>
        <th>
         Condition (
         <i>
          koşul
         </i>
         )
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         you went
        </td>

        </td>

         </i>
        </td>

         </i>
        </td>

         <i>
          gitseymişsin
         </i>
        </td>

         –
        </td>
       </tr>

        </td>

        </td>

         <i>

        </td>
       </tr>

        </td>
        <td>
         <i>

         </i>
        </td>

        </td>
       </tr>

        </td>

         </i>
        </td>

         –
        </td>

        </td>

        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       There are also so-called combined verbs, which are created by adding certain verbs (like
       <i>
        bil
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        ver
       </i>
       ) to the stem of the verb.
       <i>
        Bil
       </i>
       is the sufficiency mood. It is the equivalent of English auxiliary verbs "able to", "can" or "may".
       <i>
        Ver
       </i>
       is the swiftness,
       <i>
        kal
       </i>
       is the perpetuity and
       <i>
        yaz
       </i>
       is the approach ("almost") moods.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
        <a href="#cite_note-72">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Thus while
       <i>
        gittin
       </i>
       is "you went",
       <i>
        gidebildin
       </i>
       is "you could go" and
       <i>
        gidiverdin
       </i>
       is "you went swiftly". The tenses of the combined verbs are the same as the other verbs.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span id="Attributive_verbs_.28participles.29">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Attributive_verbs_(participles)">
        Attributive verbs (participles)
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Turkish verbs have
       <a href="/wiki/Attributive_verb" title="Attributive verb">
        attributive forms
       </a>
       , including present (with the ending
       <i>
        -en
       </i>
       ²), future (
       <i>
        -ecek
       </i>
       ²), indirect/inferential past (
       <i>
        -miş
       </i>
       <sup>
        4
       </sup>
       ), and
       <a href="/wiki/Aorist" title="Aorist">
        aorist
       </a>
       (-
       <i>
        er
       </i>
       ² or -
       <i>
        ir
       </i>
       <sup>
        4
       </sup>
       ). These forms can function as either adjectives or nouns:
       <i>
        oynamayan çocuklar
       </i>
       "children who do not play",
       <i>
        oynamayanlar
       </i>
       "those who do not play";
       <i>
        okur yazar
       </i>
       "reader-writer = literate",
       <i>
        okur yazarlar
       </i>
       "literates".
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The most important function of attributive verbs is to form modifying phrases equivalent to the
       <a href="/wiki/Relative_clause" title="Relative clause">
        relative clauses
       </a>
       found in most European languages. The attributive forms used in these constructions are the future (
       <i>
        -ecek
       </i>
       ²) and an older form (-
       <i>
        dik
       </i>
       <sup>
        4
       </sup>
       ), which covers both present and past meanings.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
        <a href="#cite_note-73">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The use of these "personal or relative participles" is illustrated in the following table, in which the examples are presented according to the grammatical case which would be seen in the equivalent English relative clause.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
        <a href="#cite_note-74">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th colspan="2">
         English equivalent
        </th>
        <th rowspan="2">
         Example
        </th>
        <th colspan="2">
         Translation
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Case of relative pronoun
        </th>
        <th>
         Pronoun
        </th>
        <th>
         Literal
        </th>
        <th>
         Idiomatic
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Nominative
        </td>
        <td>
         who, which/that
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          şimdi konuşan adam
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         "now speaking man"
        </td>
        <td>
         the man (who is) now speaking
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Genitive
        </td>
        <td>
         whose (nom.)
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          babası şimdi konuşan adam
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         "father-is now speaking man"
        </td>
        <td>
         the man whose father is now speaking
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
         whose (acc.)
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          babasını dün gördüğüm adam
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         "father-is-ACC yesterday seen-my man"
        </td>
        <td>
         the man whose father I saw yesterday
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
         at whose
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          resimlerine baktığımız ressam
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         "pictures-is-to looked-our artist"
        </td>
        <td>
         the artist whose pictures we looked at
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
         of which
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          muhtarı seçildiği köy
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         "mayor-its been-chosen-his village"
        </td>
        <td>
         the village of which he was elected mayor
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
        </td>

        </td>

         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
         the village of which he wishes to be elected mayor
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Remaining cases (incl. prepositions)
        </td>
        <td>
         whom, which
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          yazdığım mektup
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         "written-my letter"
        </td>
        <td>
         the letter (which) I wrote
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
        </td>

        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          çıktığımız kapı
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         "emerged-our door"
        </td>
        <td>
         the door from which we emerged
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
        </td>

        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          geldikleri vapur
         </i>
        </td>

        </td>
        <td>

        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>

         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         "approach-their-ACC understood-his prison days-its"
        </td>
        <td>
         the prison days (which) he knew were approaching
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
          <a href="#cite_note-75">
           [75]
          </a>
         </sup>
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
          <a href="#cite_note-76">
           [76]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Vocabulary">
        Vocabulary
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_vocabulary" title="Turkish vocabulary">
        Turkish vocabulary
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TurkishVocabulary.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/TurkishVocabulary.png/220px-TurkishVocabulary.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/TurkishVocabulary.png/330px-TurkishVocabulary.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/TurkishVocabulary.png/440px-TurkishVocabulary.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TurkishVocabulary.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Origin of the words in Turkish vocabulary, which contains 104,481 words, of which about 86% are Turkish and 14% are of foreign origin
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Latest 2010 edition of
       <i>
        Büyük Türkçe Sözlük
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        Great Turkish Dictionary
       </i>
       ), the official dictionary of the Turkish language published by Turkish Language Association, contains 616,767 words, expressions, terms and nouns.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
        <a href="#cite_note-77">
         [77]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The 2005 edition of
       <i>
        Güncel Türkçe Sözlük
       </i>
       , the official dictionary of the Turkish language published by Turkish Language Association, contains 104,481 words, of which about 86% are Turkish and 14% are of foreign origin.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dictionary_78-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Dictionary-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Among the most significant foreign contributors to Turkish vocabulary are Arabic, French, Persian, Italian, English, and Greek.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Loanwords_79-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Loanwords-79">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Word_formation">
        Word formation
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Turkish extensively uses
       <a href="/wiki/Agglutination" title="Agglutination">
        agglutination
       </a>
       to
       <a href="/wiki/Word_formation" title="Word formation">
        form new words
       </a>
       from nouns and verbal stems. The majority of Turkish words originate from the application of derivative suffixes to a relatively small set of core vocabulary.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:13_80-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:13-80">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Turkish obeys certain principles when it comes to suffixation. Most suffixes in Turkish will have more than one form, depending on the vowels and consonants in the root- vowel harmony rules will apply; consonant-initial suffixes will follow the voiced/ voiceless character of the consonant in the final unit of the root; and in the case of vowel-initial suffixes an additional consonant may be inserted if the root ends in a vowel, or the suffix may lose its initial vowel. There is also a prescribed order of affixation of suffixes- as a rule of thumb, derivative suffixes precede inflectional suffixes which are followed by
       <a href="/wiki/Clitic" title="Clitic">
        clitics
       </a>
       , as can be seen in the example set of words derived from a substantive root below:
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th>
         Turkish
        </th>
        <th>
         Components
        </th>
        <th>
         English
        </th>
        <th>
         Word class
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          göz
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          göz
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         eye
        </td>
        <td>
         Noun
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          gözlük
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          göz + -lük
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         eyeglasses
        </td>
        <td>

       <tr>

         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         optician's trade
        </td>
        <td>

        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>

         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         observation
        </td>
        <td>

        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>

         </i>

         <i>
          göz + -lem + -ci
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>

        </td>
        <td>

        </td>
       </tr>

         </i>

         </i>

         observe
        </td>

        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>

         </i>
        </td>

         <i>
          göz + -le + -mek
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         to observe
        </td>
        <td>
         Verb (infinitive)
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          gözetlemek
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          göz + -et + -le + -mek
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         to peep
        </td>
        <td>
         Verb (infinitive)
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Another example, starting from a verbal root:
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th>
         Turkish
        </th>
        <th>
         Components
        </th>
        <th>
         English
        </th>
        <th>
         Word class
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          yat-
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          yat-
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         lie down
        </td>
        <td>
         Verb (order)
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          yatmak
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          yat-mak
         </i>
        </td>

        </td>
        <td>

        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>

         </i>
        </td>

         </i>
        </td>

        </td>

        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>

        </td>

       <tr>

        </td>
        <td>
         Adjective
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>

         </i>
        </td>

          yat- + -gın
         </i>
        </td>

        </td>
        <td>
         Adjective
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          yatır-
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          yat- + -(ı)r-
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         lay down
        </td>
        <td>
         Verb (order)
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>

         </i>

         <i>

         </i>
        </td>
        <td>

        </td>
        <td>

        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>

         </i>
        </td>

        <td>

        </td>

       <tr>

         </i>
