         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         depositor, investor
        </td>
        <td>
         Noun
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       New words are also frequently formed by
       <a href="/wiki/Compound_(linguistics)" title="Compound (linguistics)">
        compounding
       </a>
       two existing words into a new one, as in German. Compounds can be of two types- bare and (s)I. The bare compounds, both nouns and adjectives are effectively two words juxtaposed without the addition of suffixes for example the word for girlfriend
       <i>
        kizarkadaş
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        kiz+arkadaş
       </i>
       ) or black pepper
       <i>
        karabiber
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        kara+biber
       </i>
       ). A few examples of compound words are given below:
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th>

        </th>

        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          pazartesi
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         Monday
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          pazar
         </i>
         ("Sunday") and
         <i>
          ertesi
         </i>
         ("after")
        </td>
        <td>
         after Sunday
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>

      <p>
       However, the majority of compound words in Turkish are (s)I compounds, which means that the second word will be marked by the 3rd person possessive suffix. A few such examples are given in the table below (note
       <a href="/wiki/Vowel_harmony" title="Vowel harmony">

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Writing_system">
        Writing system
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_alphabet" title="Turkish alphabet">
        Turkish alphabet
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_Braille" title="Turkish Braille">
        Turkish Braille
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ataturk-September_20,_1928.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="577" data-file-width="400" height="245" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Ataturk-September_20%2C_1928.jpg/170px-Ataturk-September_20%2C_1928.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Ataturk-September_20%2C_1928.jpg/255px-Ataturk-September_20%2C_1928.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Ataturk-September_20%2C_1928.jpg/340px-Ataturk-September_20%2C_1928.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ataturk-September_20,_1928.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Mustafa_Kemal_Atat%C3%BCrk" title="Mustafa Kemal Atatürk">
          Atatürk
         </a>
         introducing the new Turkish alphabet to the people of
         <a href="/wiki/Kayseri" title="Kayseri">
          Kayseri
         </a>
         . September 20, 1928. (Cover of the French
         <i>
          L'Illustration
         </i>
         magazine)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Turkish is written using a
       <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">
        Latin alphabet
       </a>
       introduced in 1928 by
       <a href="/wiki/Mustafa_Kemal_Atat%C3%BCrk" title="Mustafa Kemal Atatürk">
        Atatürk
       </a>
       to replace the
       <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Turkish_alphabet" title="Ottoman Turkish alphabet">
        Ottoman Turkish alphabet
       </a>
       , a version of
       <a href="/wiki/Persian_alphabet" title="Persian alphabet">
        Perso-Arabic
       </a>
       alphabet. The Ottoman alphabet marked only three different vowels—long
       <i>
        ā, ū
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        ī
       </i>
       —and included several redundant consonants, such as variants of
       <i>
        z
       </i>
       (which were distinguished in Arabic but not in Turkish). The omission of short vowels in the Arabic script was claimed to make it particularly unsuitable for Turkish, which has
       <a href="#Vowel_harmony">
        eight vowels
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The reform of the script was an important step in the
       <a href="/wiki/Atat%C3%BCrk%27s_Reforms" title="Atatürk's Reforms">
        cultural reforms
       </a>
       of the period. The task of preparing the new alphabet and selecting the necessary modifications for sounds specific to Turkish was entrusted to a
       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_alphabet#Modern_Turkish_alphabet" title="Turkish alphabet">
        Language Commission
       </a>
       composed of prominent linguists, academics, and writers. The introduction of the new Turkish alphabet was supported by public education centers opened throughout the country, cooperation with publishing companies, and encouragement by Atatürk himself, who toured the country teaching the new letters to the public.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dilacar_81-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Dilacar-81">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As a result, there was a dramatic increase in literacy from its original Third World levels.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
        <a href="#cite_note-82">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Latin alphabet was applied to the Turkish language for educational purposes even before the 20th-century reform. Instances include a 1635 Latin-Albanian dictionary by
       <a href="/wiki/Frang_Bardhi" title="Frang Bardhi">
        Frang Bardhi
       </a>
       , who also incorporated several sayings in the Turkish language, as an appendix to his work (e.g.
       <i>
        alma agatsdan irak duschamas
       </i>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
        <a href="#cite_note-83">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
       —"An apple does not fall far from its tree").
      </p>
      <p>
       Turkish now has an alphabet suited to the sounds of the language: the spelling is largely
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phonemic_spelling" title="Phonemic spelling">
        phonemic
       </a>
       , with one letter corresponding to each
       <a href="/wiki/Phoneme" title="Phoneme">
        phoneme
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       Most of the letters are used approximately as in English, the main exceptions being ⟨c⟩, which denotes
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [dʒ]
       </span>
       (⟨j⟩ being used for the
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ʒ]
       </span>
       found in Persian and European loans); and the undotted ⟨ı⟩, representing
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɯ]
       </span>
       . As in German, ⟨ö⟩ and ⟨ü⟩ represent
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ø]
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [y]
       </span>
       . The letter ⟨ğ⟩, in principle, denotes
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɣ]
       </span>
       but has the property of lengthening the preceding vowel and assimilating any subsequent vowel. The letters ⟨ş⟩ and ⟨ç⟩ represent
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ʃ]
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [tʃ]
       </span>
       , respectively. A
       <a href="/wiki/Circumflex" title="Circumflex">
        circumflex
       </a>
       is written over
       <a href="/wiki/Back_vowel" title="Back vowel">
        back vowels
       </a>
       following ⟨k⟩, ⟨g⟩, or ⟨l⟩ when these consonants represent
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [c]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɟ]
       </span>
       , and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [l]
       </span>
       —almost exclusively in Arabic and Persian
       <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">
        loans
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
        <a href="#cite_note-84">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
       An
       <a href="/wiki/Apostrophe" title="Apostrophe">
        apostrophe
       </a>
       is used to separate
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proper_nouns" title="Proper nouns">
        proper nouns
       </a>
       from inflectional suffixes: e.g.
       <i>
        <span lang="tr">
         <i>
          İstanbul'da
         </i>
        </span>
       </i>
       "in Istanbul"' (but not from derivational suffixes, e.g.
       <i>
        <span lang="tr">
         <i>
          İstanbullu
         </i>
        </span>
       </i>
       "from/of Istanbul").
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Turkish alphabet consists of 29 letters (q, x, w omitted and ç, ş, ğ, ı, ö, ü added); the complete list is:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         a, b, c, ç, d, e, f, g, ğ, h, ı, i, j, k, l, m, n, o, ö, p, r, s, ş, t, u, ü, v, y
        </i>
        , and
        <i>
         z
        </i>
        (Note that capital of
        <i>
         i
        </i>
        is
        <i>
         İ
        </i>
        and lowercase
        <i>
         I
        </i>
        is
        <i>
         ı
        </i>
        .)
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       The specifically Turkish letters and spellings described above are illustrated in this table:
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th>

        </th>
       </tr>

         <i>

            </a>
           </i>

         </i>
        </td>

         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          ˈdʒaːɫoːɫu
         </span>

        </td>
       </tr>

       </tr>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Sample">
        Sample
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       <i>
        Dostlar Beni Hatırlasın
       </i>
       by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A%C5%9F%C4%B1k_Veysel_%C5%9Eat%C4%B1ro%C4%9Flu" title="Aşık Veysel Şatıroğlu">
        Aşık Veysel Şatıroğlu
       </a>
       (1894–1973), a
       <a href="/wiki/Minstrel" title="Minstrel">
        minstrel
       </a>
       and highly regarded poet in the
       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_folk_literature" title="Turkish folk literature">
        Turkish folk literature
       </a>
       tradition.
      </p>
      <table border="0" style="width:100%; font-family:serif; font-size:115%; text-align:center;">
       <tr>
        <th style="width:25%;">
         Orthography
        </th>
        <th style="width:35%;">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">
          IPA
         </a>
        </th>
        <th style="width:40%;">
         Translation
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Ben giderim adım kalır
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          bæn ɟid̪e̞ɾim äd̪ɯm käɫɯɾ
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         I depart, my name remains
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Dostlar beni hatırlasın
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          d̪o̞st̪ɫäɾ be̞ni hätɯɾɫäsɯn
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         May friends remember me
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Düğün olur bayram gelir
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          d̪yjyn o̞ɫuɾ bäjɾäm ɟe̞liɾ
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         There are weddings, there are feasts
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Dostlar beni hatırlasın
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          d̪o̞st̪ɫäɾ be̞ni hätɯɾɫäsɯn
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         May friends remember me
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <br/>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Can kafeste durmaz uçar
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Turkish_Whistling_Language">
        Turkish Whistling Language
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       For more details on whistling as a language, see
       <a href="/wiki/Whistled_language" title="Whistled language">
        Whistled language
       </a>
       .
      </div>
      <p>
       In the Turkish province of
       <a href="/wiki/Giresun_Province" title="Giresun Province">
        Giresun
       </a>
       , the locals in the village of
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ku%C5%9Fk%C3%B6y&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Kuşköy (page does not exist)">
        Kuşköy
       </a>
       have communicated using a whistled version of Turkish for over 400 years. The region consists of a series of deep valleys and the unusual mode of communication allows for conversation over distances of up to 5 kilometres. Turkish authorities estimate that there are still around 10,000 people using the whistled language. However, in 2011
       <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">
        UNESCO
       </a>
       found whistling Turkish to be a dying language and included it in its
       <a href="/wiki/UNESCO_Intangible_Cultural_Heritage_Lists" title="UNESCO Intangible Cultural Heritage Lists">
        intangible cultural heritage list
       </a>
       . Since then the local education directorate has introduced it as a course in schools in the region, hoping to revive its use.
      </p>
      <p>
       A study was conducted by a German scientist of origin
       <a href="/wiki/Onur_G%C3%BCnt%C3%BCrk%C3%BCn" title="Onur Güntürkün">
        Onur Güntürkün
       </a>
       at Ruhr University, observing 31 "speakers" of
       <i>
        <span lang="tr">
         kuş dili
        </span>
       </i>
       ("bird's tongue") from Kuşköy, and he found that the whistled language mirrored the lexical and syntactical structure of Turkish language.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
        <a href="#cite_note-85">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>

          <span class="reference-text">
           The name TDK itself exemplifies this process. The words
           <i>
            tetkik
           </i>
           and
           <i>
            cemiyet
           </i>
           in the original name are both Arabic loanwords (the final
           <i>
            -i
           </i>
           of
           <i>
            cemiyeti
           </i>
           being a Turkish possessive suffix);
           <i>
            kurum
           </i>
           is a native Turkish word based on the verb
           <i>
            kurmak
           </i>
           , "set up, found".
           <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
            [
            <i>
             <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
              <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2014)">
               citation needed
              </span>
             </a>
            </i>
            ]
           </sup>
          </span>
         </li>

            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           Note that this table is essentially the same as the IPA vowel chart shown above: both table and chart indicate the physical location and quality of each vowel. However, the second table includes additional information on how Turkish harmonies vowels sounds across syllables based on the physical location and quality of the initial syllable.

         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-48">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-48">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           Lewis (1953):21
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-49">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-49">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           For the terms
           <i>
            twofold
           </i>
           and
           <i>
            fourfold
           </i>
           , as well as the superscript notation, see Lewis (1953):21–22. In his more recent works Lewis prefers to omit the superscripts, on the grounds that "there is no need for this once the principle has been grasped" (Lewis [2001]:18).
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-50">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-50">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           In modern Turkish orthography, an apostrophe is used to separate proper names from any suffixes.
          </span>
         </li>

         <li id="cite_note-57">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-57">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           This section draws heavily on Lewis (2001) and, to a lesser extent, Lewis (1953). Only the most important references are specifically flagged with footnotes.
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-58">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-58">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>

           see Lewis (2001) Ch XIV.
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-59">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-59">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           "The prefix, which is accented, is modelled on the first syllable of the simple adjective or adverb but with the substitution of
           <b>
            m, p, r
           </b>
           , or
           <b>
            s
           </b>
           for the last consonant of that syllable." Lewis (2001):55. The prefix retains the first vowel of the base form and thus exhibits a form of reverse vowel harmony.
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-60">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-60">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           This "splendid word" appeared at the time of
           <i>
            Bayram
           </i>
           , the festival marking the end of the
           <a href="/wiki/Ramadan" title="Ramadan">
            month of fasting
           </a>
           . Lewis (2001):287.
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-61">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">

            <a href="#cite_ref-61">

            <u>

            </u>

         <li id="cite_note-73">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-73">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           See Lewis (2001):163–165, 260–262 for an exhaustive treatment.
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-74">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-74">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           For the terms
           <i>
            personal
           </i>
           and
           <i>
            relative
           </i>
           participle see Lewis (1958):98 and Lewis (2001):163 respectively. Most of the examples are taken from Lewis (2001).
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-75">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-75">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           This more complex example from
           <a href="/wiki/Orhan_Pamuk" title="Orhan Pamuk">
            Orhan Pamuk
           </a>
           's
           <i>
            Kar
           </i>
           (
           <i>
            <a href="/wiki/Snow_(Pamuk_novel)" title="Snow (Pamuk novel)">
             Snow
            </a>
           </i>
           ) contains a nested structure: [
           <i>
            which he knew
           </i>
           [
           <i>
            were approaching
           </i>
           ]].
           <a href="/wiki/Maureen_Freely" title="Maureen Freely">
            Maureen Freely
           </a>
           's more succinct and idiomatic translation is
           <i>
            the days in prison he knew lay ahead
           </i>
           . Note that Pamuk uses the spelling
           <i>
            hapisane
           </i>
           .
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-76">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-76">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           From the perspective of Turkish grammar
           <i>
            yaklaştığını anladığı
           </i>
           is exactly parallel to
           <i>
            babasını gördüğüm
           </i>
           ("whose father I saw"), and could therefore be paraphrased as "whose approaching he understood".
          </span>
         </li>
